        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer48460343" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14685077035267120991">I read this book after finishing her <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18490.Frankenstein' title='Frankenstein by Mary Shelley' rel='nofollow noopener'>collected short stories</a> and Wise Blood, her first novel. None of her other work prepared me for this, her cynical, paranoid mind fuck of a novel. Her prose, as always, is clear and excellent, and she once again explores faith and its absence in a way that allows for no easy solutions, no pat answers. But Christ on a crutch is this a difficult book. One of the things I love about her work is the wry sense of humor laced through it, balancing out the dark theme</span>
  <span id="freeText14685077035267120991" style="display:none">I read this book after finishing her <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18490.Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein by Mary Shelley" rel="nofollow noopener">collected short stories</a> and Wise Blood, her first novel. None of her other work prepared me for this, her cynical, paranoid mind fuck of a novel. Her prose, as always, is clear and excellent, and she once again explores faith and its absence in a way that allows for no easy solutions, no pat answers. But Christ on a crutch is this a difficult book. One of the things I love about her work is the wry sense of humor laced through it, balancing out the dark themes and bursts of violence. That humor is unfortunately missing from 'Violent', making for a grim read about two wholly unlikeable characters who spend most of the book in a power struggle over the soul of a young mentally challenged boy. While there are some wonderful moments -O'Connor once again proves that she is the master of the 'telling detail'- all in all I found this a troubling, exhausting read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14685077035267120991" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer12979458815403903208">I read this because it was a book choice of a group I am in and because I had never read O’Connor before. I have just finished it and will write a longer review but want to say here that I was absolutely blown away by the author’s writing which has brilliance and power. I now intend to read everything by this author.<br/><br/>I think what made it so good for me was that I listened to it as an audiobook. It is definitely not an easy read and is not one I would normally read but the writing and the depth of</span>
  <span id="freeText12979458815403903208" style="display:none">I read this because it was a book choice of a group I am in and because I had never read O’Connor before. I have just finished it and will write a longer review but want to say here that I was absolutely blown away by the author’s writing which has brilliance and power. I now intend to read everything by this author.<br /><br />I think what made it so good for me was that I listened to it as an audiobook. It is definitely not an easy read and is not one I would normally read but the writing and the depth of the message that I think Flannery was trying to convey was very powerful. <br /><br />Flannery had lost her father to Lupus and had it herself. I think she saw life as being filled with violence and yet she still had faith. This is the power behind it.<br /><br />This quote from A Prayer Journal I think sums up what Flannery was referring to:<br /><br />'Sin is a great thing if recognized. It leads a good many people to God who wouldn't get there otherwise.' <br /></span>

<div id="review_116764736" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/116764736" />
    <a title="Skylar Burris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/710201-skylar-burris"><img alt="Skylar Burris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1449959508p2/710201.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/116764736?book_show_action=true">Aug 14, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Skylar Burris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Skylar Burris" href="/user/show/710201-skylar-burris">Skylar Burris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer116764736" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4730043536046453110">I was struck by this book, but I feel I have not yet understood it. As I read this, I kept thinking of O'Connor's remark in an interview that the south, if not exactly Christ-centered, is "Christ haunted." This is a disturbing, difficult book, and I was left wondering, "What does it all mean?" O’Connor reminds me of another Catholic author, Graham Greene, in her depiction of the Christian religious person as sinful, dark, violent, passionate, vibrantly alive, touched and consumed and overpowered</span>
  <span id="freeText4730043536046453110" style="display:none">I was struck by this book, but I feel I have not yet understood it. As I read this, I kept thinking of O'Connor's remark in an interview that the south, if not exactly Christ-centered, is "Christ haunted." This is a disturbing, difficult book, and I was left wondering, "What does it all mean?" O’Connor reminds me of another Catholic author, Graham Greene, in her depiction of the Christian religious person as sinful, dark, violent, passionate, vibrantly alive, touched and consumed and overpowered at moments by the power of God or of grace, as juxtaposed with the secular (or the bourgeois or the new age) person who is blandly respectable, does no great evil (or great good), is wishy-washy, lukewarm, void of meaningful emotional depths. This contrast is so stark in the writing of both authors that it makes the religious Christian figures (as awful as they are) appear amazingly substantive against the insubstantial shells that are the non-religious characters. It is a way of powerfully depicting the substance of the spiritual realm, a reality that is more real than real, that is so real that it makes everything that does not allow itself to be touched by it seem hollow. I don't know how I feel about this take on Christianity. I see some truth in it ("Be ye either hot or cold or I will spew you out of my mouth"), but at the same time it is disturbing, and it leaves no room for a quiet, joyful, commonplace Christianity. When I read these types of books, I am struck for days with a kind of image of them, with an emotional imprint, and at the same time I do not like them. I do not like them and yet they feel strangely real, strangely true, in a sense I can't quite penatrate with mere reason. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4730043536046453110" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1160350474500161733" style="display:none">When it comes to religion, I find O'Connor much like Dostoevsky in that she is able to present a series of characters and situations that to me very forcefully illustrate the non-existence of God, while I'm certain she creates them to argue the complete opposite.<br /><br />So, spoiler alert I guess. Francis Marion Tarwater, a fourteen year old sociopathic (and possibly schizophrenic) murderer with delusions of biblical grandeur is not reached whatsoever by the secular-minded uncle who attempts to help him overcome his incredibly bizarre upbringing. In the final part of the book, he is raped, starts setting fire to everything he comes across, and is eventually cleansed by the flames and redeemed as a prophet of God. Yeah.<br /><br />It's a beautiful book, a sickening book, in which rage, violence and desperation fairly leap off the page. The first sentence is pure gold. And when I read the last three paragraphs, I thought the book might burst into flames in my hand.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1448886750" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1448886750" />
    <a title="Tom LA" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4252261-tom-la"><img alt="Tom LA" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578866627p2/4252261.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1448886750?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom LA" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom LA" href="/user/show/4252261-tom-la">Tom LA</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1448886750&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1448886750" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer451593092735627349">"The violent bear it away", a novel that's been categorized as "Southern Gothic", is written with elegance and with an intensity verging on anger. You know that buzzing noise you hear when walking below those overhead high-voltage power lines? And the buzzing feels like it's getting to your skin? This novel is like that (like everything I've read of O'Connor so far): charged up with a LOT of electricity. <br/><br/>It's an allegorical tale, every character a symbol or a metaphor for something clearly defin</span>
  <span id="freeText451593092735627349" style="display:none">"The violent bear it away", a novel that's been categorized as "Southern Gothic", is written with elegance and with an intensity verging on anger. You know that buzzing noise you hear when walking below those overhead high-voltage power lines? And the buzzing feels like it's getting to your skin? This novel is like that (like everything I've read of O'Connor so far): charged up with a LOT of electricity. <br /><br />It's an allegorical tale, every character a symbol or a metaphor for something clearly defined: religious zeal, modern rationalism, and the modern man of the '50s, who is struggling between the two, while walking in a very dark world. <br /><br />At times, it gave me a very "Catcher in the Rye" feeling, of course the Southern Gothic version of it, but that same visceral pull, a kind of deep, raw fascination conveyed by the intensity and the conflicted confusion of adolescence. <br /><br />But it just doesn't work. Both as a parable and as a story, I found this novel to be a bloody mess.<br /><br />The first part was originally a short story, and I would have preferred it that way. I loved the crazy back and forth dialogue between the stocky great uncle and young Tarwater. The scene where the uncle is laying in his own coffin, with only his protruding belly popping out, barking orders at his nephew about the details of his burial, is a fantastic little surreal piece.<br /><br />Unfortunately, somehow this work became a novel where, as odd as it sounds, the mentally ill have the responsibility to defend the validity of the Christian faith. <br /><br />The story presents a cast of charachters who go from abusive to dangerously sociopathic. Of course, they cause themselves and each other a lot of harm. O'Connor used to go church every day, and she used her writing to preach. Towards the end of this novel, she wants the reader to forget about her characters' mental illness, and even to forget that one of them is an unconvicted murderer. <br /><br />That is what doesn't work. Allegories are better placed in religious texts - and there are already more than enough. To the reader in search for a good novel, these characters who represent ideas become dull very soon. Everything happens in this kind of magic-realism haze, but if you don't know that you should take the characters as symbols, you end up very confused. <br /><br />What you are supposed to do is swallow the whole thing as a parable, the weirdest and darkest parable you've ever heard. <br /><br />O'Connor has an endless reservoir of anger for secular modernity, psychology and rationalism. All these find their incarnation in the character of the School Teacher, against whom she unleashes her own personal fury: the poor guy is constantly irritated, furious, angry and lives a life of pain because he doesn't allow himself to love. <br /><br />The equation secularity/rationalism = refusal to love is never articulated, and I find it very weak. <br /><br />In any case, there you go: if you don't follow O'Connor orthodox, rigid and quite angry type of catholic faith, you are unable to love. Wow. What a disappointingly narrow-minded view of non-christian people. <br /><br />Let's hear it from the author herself, from an interview: "Writers who see by the light of their Christian faith will have, in these times, the sharpest eye for the grotesque, for the perverse, and for the unacceptable…. To the hard of hearing you shout, and for the almost-blind you draw large and startling figures".<br /><br />How sad and delusional is that? Did she seriously mean to evangelize to the blind and deaf non-believers with this disturbing, obscure oddness of a book?<br /><br />Her "large and startling figures" had the complete opposite effect on me: I didn't take anything of what they were saying seriously, because they were total nutters. Only towards the end you realize that O'Connor was actually siding with the great uncle-nutcase all along. Yes, the great man who messed up the lives of two nephews with his insanity, and even attempted to murder one of them. To me, that just doesn't work.<br /><br />So I did some research, and I was glad to discover that O'Connor HERSELF was unconvinced about this novel, even after she had finished it. (I really should have read her short stories instead). <br /><br />This is from Paul Elie’s book, "An American Pilgrimage", describing Flannery’s struggle to write and to finish this book:<br /><br />“She was calling it The Violent Bear It Away, and the title suggested the struggle of writing it. She saw it as a book about baptism, and thought the challenge was to get the reader to see baptism as a matter of life and death. She had to “bend the whole novel” so that the baptism carried “enough awe and mystery to jar the reader into some kind of emotional recognition of its significance.<br /><br />“So she bent the novel toward what she supposed were the modern reader’s sympathies. It would be a novel of initiation, about the boy’s entry into manhood, a motif the reader would recognize. It would begin and end with a religious ritual: in the beginning the ritual of death, the boy’s burial of his great uncle, who is a prophet, and in the end the ritual of life, the baptism of his nephew, who is mentally challenged. It would lead the reader to see these rituals in the way that she saw them herself – baptism as the initiation into the life of grace, and burial as the initiation into the life of eternity.<br /><br />“Thus described, her approach has a Jamesian elegance, but in practice it proved so unwieldy that simply keeping the story straight became her biggest challenge. <br /><br />“She finished in January 1959, nearly seven years after she started. “I am more and more satisfied with the title and less and less satisfied with the rest of it", she told Hester. To the Fitzgeralds she wrote, “I cannot see it any longer and the only thing I can determine about it is that nobody else would have wanted to write it but me."<br /><br />She could not see it any longer, and I certainly cannot see it today in 2015! <br /><br />There is an incredible talent in her writing though. Let's go grab her short stories.</span>
  <a data-text-id="451593092735627349" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=american-lit">american-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=1960-s">1960-s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=read-in-2019">read-in-2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=haunting-and-spectrality">haunting-and-spectrality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=religion-and-mysticism">religion-and-mysticism</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2991401887" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12522469122072453011">Second reading. A dark, inevitably violent novel; the physical violence is occasional, but the spiritual antagonism is incessant and oppressive. The undeniably gorgeous writing animates a deeply apocalyptic vision: O'Connor presents a fallen world where the devil walks among humanity and prophecy is the genuine calling of the elect. It brought back powerful memories of reading the Old Testament as a child and taking its stories as historical fact deeply applicable to my own life, the incontrover</span>
  <span id="freeText12522469122072453011" style="display:none">Second reading. A dark, inevitably violent novel; the physical violence is occasional, but the spiritual antagonism is incessant and oppressive. The undeniably gorgeous writing animates a deeply apocalyptic vision: O'Connor presents a fallen world where the devil walks among humanity and prophecy is the genuine calling of the elect. It brought back powerful memories of reading the Old Testament as a child and taking its stories as historical fact deeply applicable to my own life, the incontrovertible proof that my unremarkable everyday reality was a mere curtain separating me from an ancient high-stakes battle waged across both the earthly and spiritual realms. <br /><br />I don't at all miss inhabiting this world but find it helpful to revisit such terrain on occasion. I couldn't help but feel on this rereading it captures an aspect of the American psyche that feels eerily familiar again, having reemerged with overwhelming dominance in our current historical moment.<br /><br /><i>"The sky was a bright pink, casting such a weird light that every color was intensified. Each weed that grew out of the gravel looked like a live green nerve. The world might have been shedding its own skin."</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="12522469122072453011" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_589479947" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/589479947" />
    <a title="S Suzanne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13041326-s-suzanne"><img alt="S Suzanne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1366027344p2/13041326.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/589479947?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="S Suzanne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="S Suzanne" href="/user/show/13041326-s-suzanne">S Suzanne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13041326-s-suzanne?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13041326-s-suzanne?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer589479947" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6491086462976162361">I grew up in the Bible Belt, the "Christ-haunted" south...and I agree with some others here that FOC makes the religious zealots burn off the page, and the non-believers (or those fighting not to believe)are depicted as empty husks.<br/><br/>It is a strange perspective to be a passionate Catholic (as FOC was) in the Bible Belt. <br/><br/>Schizoid, Gothic, and occasionally beautiful are words that come to mind for the reactions of young tender minds brought up "in Jesus" in the South. (While I was surrounded by this</span>
  <span id="freeText6491086462976162361" style="display:none">I grew up in the Bible Belt, the "Christ-haunted" south...and I agree with some others here that FOC makes the religious zealots burn off the page, and the non-believers (or those fighting not to believe)are depicted as empty husks.<br /><br />It is a strange perspective to be a passionate Catholic (as FOC was) in the Bible Belt. <br /><br />Schizoid, Gothic, and occasionally beautiful are words that come to mind for the reactions of young tender minds brought up "in Jesus" in the South. (While I was surrounded by this, I myself was not brought up this way.) <br /><br />Her characters seem far-out, but I felt they were real enough beyond puppeted symbols. The very quality of her writing expresses the compelling draw of religious passion. I have had three close male friends who have definitely had a fiery imprint on them from a very young age, for good or ill. All three have feared what "their blood" may draw them toward. One was getting "raised up" to preach and ended up in a punk rock band drenched in blood...and writhing in pain and pleasure at guilt. One was a cerebral and passionate atheist who turned to Christ in his last year, even playing Jesus in a church play, grotesquely covered in blood, as I hear it. One is a professor who teaches humanities. His family background was torn from the pages of FOC and Faulkner. All 3 are gen-X guys who grew up with Jesus, and consumed Faulkner and FOC. They wrestled with their origin.<br /><br />As a child, I would think a book like this was trying to be as wild as possible. Now I feel it is heightened realism. The main place I felt the strain was with the uncle who was so worried he might revert. But some people have no middle road left when they feel they are tearing themselves away from a cult.<br /><br />I will be interested to read more of FOC's personal beliefs. Part of what makes her so compelling to me is that she has her own very specific beliefs and points to her stories, whether that's what we take away or not.<br /><br />She is a great writer, and this book was enjoyable to read for the dark and unique poetry of her storytelling, even if it was tormenting in other ways. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6491086462976162361" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1616051350" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1616051350" />
    <a title="Cody" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48027559-cody"><img alt="Cody" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507665393p2/48027559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1616051350?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cody" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cody" href="/user/show/48027559-cody">Cody</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/13067179-the-violent-bear-it-away">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48027559-cody?shelf=favoritism">favoritism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48027559-cody?shelf=hillbilly-bats">hillbilly-bats</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1616051350" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2089542969454528702">Holy shit!<br/><br/>No, seriously, this is some holy shit: it’s Flannery O’Connor, so it kind of goes with the territory. Holy holy crazy Biblical doomsday abounds and it is fantastic. Idamn it, God! Why’d you have to fell so incredible a writer at only 39? This, her final novel, only hints at the potentiality that we were denied. Oh well, I guess an angel got its wings or whatever the saying is. Or is that every time a baby cries?<br/><br/>If you dug Hazel Motes or any of the guano-sniffers from <i>Good Man</i>, this is </span>
  <span id="freeText2089542969454528702" style="display:none">Holy shit!<br /><br />No, seriously, this is some holy shit: it’s Flannery O’Connor, so it kind of goes with the territory. Holy holy crazy Biblical doomsday abounds and it is fantastic. Idamn it, God! Why’d you have to fell so incredible a writer at only 39? This, her final novel, only hints at the potentiality that we were denied. Oh well, I guess an angel got its wings or whatever the saying is. Or is that every time a baby cries?<br /><br />If you dug Hazel Motes or any of the guano-sniffers from <i>Good Man</i>, this is right up your sado-Catholic alley. As ever, this is simply pitiless. The prose, sparse as always, is perfectly attenuated to the subject matter. Density would be antithetical to O’Connor’s <i> raison d’etre</i>: to bludgeon the brain out of your ears with unflinching honesty in the form of a Bible-shaped hammer. More, please!<br /><br />That said, the authorial maturity that took place between this and <i>Wise Blood</i> is astounding. To think this was only her second novel blows my speakers. Extrapolating ‘what could have been’ can be found in <i>Everything That Rises Must Converge</i> or any of the other posthumous cobbling sessions her publishers got off on compiling for your judiciously leavened bread. But this is it as far as the long form novel, kids. What a shadow to cast. Looks suspiciously like a crucifix. <br /><br />Personally, I’m looking forward to the Resurrection so O’Connor can write <i>The Violent Bear It Away II: Tarwater in the City</i>. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2089542969454528702" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('7ZrW+WN4yUCDlQST3NoZ0uqOaqqbJp9q70RxyTbrUBmdtp9sNbG2CgRHxdBfVjlyWtNHSZQT55lrToe9x1dWZQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gip9SsykwzNoLlVgEUf+4vJB7zAKxSUUiK010+6UsJjyBjTfmm28ee/8lCOSy95CQhzC0wXwXecMp8OnHyi25A==')})
  }

Liberal and like-minded-editors, writers, a decorator, a theater producer, and one financial guy, Eva\'s husband, Bruce-the friends have come to the countryside in the hope of restoring the bubble in which they have grown used to living. Yet with the exception of one brash and obnoxious book editor, none is willing to accept Eva\'s challenge.Shelter in Place is a novel about house and home, furniture and rooms, safety and freedom and the invidious ways in which political upheaval can undermine even the most seemingly impregnable foundations. Eva is the novel\'s polestar, a woman who moves through her days accompanied by a roving, carefully curated salon. She\'s a generous hostess and more than a bit of a control freak, whose obsession with decorating allows Leavitt to treat us to a slyly comic look at the habitués and fetishes of the so-called shelter industry. Yet when, in her avidity to secure shelter for herself, she persuades Bruce to buy a grand if dilapidated apartment in Venice, she unwittingly sets off the chain of events that will propel him, for the first time, to venture outside the bubble and embark on a wholly unexpected love affair.\n\nA comic portrait of the months immediately following the 2016 election, Shelter in Place is also a Brad Gooch brings to life O\'Connor\'s significant friendships -- with Robert Lowell, Elizabeth Hardwick, Walker Percy, and James Dickey among others -- and her deeply felt convictions, as expressed in her communications with Thomas Merton, Elizabeth Bishop, and Betty Hester. Hester was famously known as &quot;A&quot; in O\'Connor\'s collected letters, The Habit of Being, and a large cache of correspondence to her from O\'Connor was made available to scholars, including Brad Gooch, in 2006. O\'Connor\'s capacity to live fully -- despite the chronic disease that eventually confined her to her mother\'s farm in Georgia -- is illuminated in this engaging and authoritative biography. Chesterton<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.02 avg rating &mdash; 680 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1922\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer8555236755376112507\">In the second decade of the twentieth century, an idea became all too fashionable among those who feel it is their right to set social trends. Wealthy families took it on as a pet cause, generously bankrolling its research. The New York Times praised<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText8555236755376112507\" style=\"display:none\">In the second decade of the twentieth century, an idea became all too fashionable among those who feel it is their right to set social trends. Wealthy families took it on as a pet cause, generously bankrolling its research. The New York Times praised it as a wonderful &quot;new science.&quot; Scientists, such as the brilliant plant biologist, Luther Burbank, praised it unashamedly. Educators as prominent as Charles Elliot, President of Harvard University, promoted it as a solution to social ills. America\'s public schools did their part. In the 1920s, almost three-fourths of high school social science textbooks taught its principles. Not to be outdone, judges and physicians called for those principles to be enshrined into law. Congress agree, passing the 1924 immigration law to exclude from American shores the people of Eastern and Southern Europe that the idea branded as inferior. In 1927, the U. S. Supreme Court joined the chorus, ruling by a lopsided vote of 8 to 1 that the sterilization of unwilling men and women was constitutional. That idea was eugenics and in the English-speaking world it had virtually no critics among the &quot;chattering classes.&quot; When he wrote this book, Chesterton stood virtually alone against the intellectual world of his day. Yet to his eternal credit, he showed no sign of being intimidated by the prestige of his foes. On the contrary, he thunders against eugenics, ranking it one of the great evils of modern society. And, in perhaps one of the most chillingly accurate prophecies of the century, he warns that the ideas that eugenics had unleashed were likely to bear bitter fruit in another nation. That nation was Germany, the &quot;very land of scientific culture from which the ideal of a Superman had come.&quot; In fact, the very group that Nazism tried to exterminate, Eastern European Jews, and the group it targeted for later extermination, the Slavs, were two of those whose biological unfitness eugenists sought so eagerly to confirm.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"8555236755376112507\" But perhaps the most shocking thing about Flannery O’Connor’s fiction is the fact that it is shaped by a thoroughly Christian vision. If the world she depicts is dark and terrifying, it is also the place where grace makes itself known. Her world—our world—is the stage whereon the divine comedy plays out; the freakishness and violence in O’Connor’s stories, so often mistaken for a kind of misanthropy or even nihilism, turn out to be a call to mercy.\n\nIn this biography, Jonathan Rogers gets at the heart of O’Connor’s work. He follows the roots of her fervent Catholicism and traces the outlines of a life marked by illness and suffering, but ultimately defined by an irrepressible joy and even hilarity. In her stories, and in her life story, Flannery O’Connor extends a hand in the dark, warning and reassuring us of the terrible speed of mercy.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"7657630583715394950\" What follows is an adventure through the Harrikin, an eerie backwoods filled with tangled roads, rusted machinery, and eccentric squatters–old men, witches, and families among them–who both shield and imperil Tyler as he runs for safety. With his poetic, haunting prose, William Gay rewrites the rules of the gothic fairy tale while exploring the classic Southern themes of good and evil.<\/span>\n  <a <script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_53438190'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53438190-live-not-by-lies?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Live Not by Lies: A Manual for Christian Dissidents<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/125790.Rod_Dreher?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Rod Dreher<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.39 avg rating &mdash; 405 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published \n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer1032071122515882632\">The New York Times bestselling author of The Benedict Option draws on the wisdom of Christian survivors of Soviet persecution to warn American Christians of approaching dangers.\n\nFor years, émigrés from the former Soviet bloc have been telling Rod Dr<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1032071122515882632\" style=\"display:none\">The New York Times bestselling author of The Benedict Option draws on the wisdom of Christian survivors of Soviet persecution to warn American Christians of approaching dangers.\n\nFor years, émigrés from the former Soviet bloc have been telling Rod Dreher they see telltale signs of &quot;soft&quot; totalitarianism cropping up in America--something more Brave New World than Nineteen Eighty-Four. Identity politics are beginning to encroach on every aspect of life. Civil liberties are increasingly seen as a threat to &quot;safety&quot;. Progressives marginalize conservative, traditional Christians, and other dissenters. Technology and consumerism hasten the possibility of a corporate surveillance state. And the pandemic, having put millions out of work, leaves our country especially vulnerable to demagogic manipulation.\n\nIn Live Not By Lies, Dreher amplifies the alarm sounded by the brave men and women who fought totalitarianism. He explains how the totalitarianism facing us today is based less on overt violence and more on psychological manipulation. He tells the stories of modern-day dissidents--clergy, laity, martyrs, and confessors from the Soviet Union and the captive nations of Europe--who offer practical advice for how to identify and resist totalitarianism in our time. Following the model offered by a prophetic World War II-era pastor who prepared believers in his Eastern European to endure the coming of communism, Live Not By Lies teaches American Christians a method for resistance:  - SEE: Acknowledge the reality of the situation. - JUDGE: Assess reality in the light of what we as Christians know to be true. - ACT: Take action to protect truth.\n\nAleksandr Solzhenitsyn famously said that one of the biggest mistakes people make is assuming totalitarianism can\'t happen in their country. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6284038342573822261"><b>Flannery O'Connor</b> was born in Savannah, Georgia, in 1925. When she died at the age of thirty-nine, America lost one of its most gifted writers at the height of her powers. O’Connor wrote two novels, Wise Blood (1952) and The Violent Bear It Away (1960), and two story collections, A Good Man Is Hard to Find (1955) and Everything That Rises Must Converge (1964). Her Complete Stories, published posth</span>
  <span id="freeText6284038342573822261" style="display:none"><b>Flannery O'Connor</b> was born in Savannah, Georgia, in 1925. When she died at the age of thirty-nine, America lost one of its most gifted writers at the height of her powers. O’Connor wrote two novels, Wise Blood (1952) and The Violent Bear It Away (1960), and two story collections, A Good Man Is Hard to Find (1955) and Everything That Rises Must Converge (1964). Her Complete Stories, published posthumously in 1972, won the National Book Award that year, and in a 2009 online poll it was voted as the best book to have won the award in the contest’s 60-year history. Her essays were published in Mystery and Manners (1969) and her letters in The Habit of Being (1979). In 1988 the Library of America published her Collected Works; she was the first postwar writer to be so honored. O’Connor was educated at the Georgia State College for Women, studied writing at the Iowa Writers’ Workshop, and wrote much of Wise Blood at the Yaddo artists’ colony in upstate New York. She lived most of her adult life on her family’s ancestral farm, Andalusia, outside Milledgeville, Georgia</span>
  <a data-text-id="6284038342573822261" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
